                     <h1 class="Font-A-6-110 title W-100" id="article-aft-title-120050986797">
                      Study: Tattoos Can Cause Serious, Long-Term, Adverse Reactions
                     </h1>
                     <div class="attribution-component D-b W-100" id="article-aft-attribution-120050986797" style="max-width:305px;">

                          <cite class="byline col D-b Fs-n" style="padding-bottom:15px;">
                           <span class="author-name Font-C-2-140 D-b">
                            <a class="attribution-name Font-C-2-140 Fw-b Whs-n" data-ylk="t1:a2;t2:article;t3:cat;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:17;elm:itm;elmt:link;itc:0" href="https://twitter.com/msgracegold" style="color:#000;" target="_blank">

                            </a>
                           </span>
                           <time class="date Font-C-2-140 Fw-n D-b">
                            <span>
                             May 28, 2015
                            </span>
                           </time>
                          </cite>
                         </div>
                        </div>
                       </div>
                      </div>
                     </div>
                    </div>
                    <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-right" style="max-width:500px;">
                     <div class="text-body-wrapper W-100 Font-B-1-160">
                      <noscript>
                      </noscript>
                      <div class="text-body W-100 Font-B-1-160" id="article-aft-text-body-120050986797" style="color:#222222;max-width:500px;">
                       <div class="body read-more-cut">
                        <div class="pre-cut" style="height:auto;margin-bottom:1.5em;">
                         <figure>
                          <img alt="Study: Tattoos Can Cause Serious, Long-Term, Adverse Reactions" height="540" src="https://s.yimg.com/cd/resizer/2.0/FIT_TO_WIDTH-w540/13d7c4eccc3abebf6f93904fa38138c7711ce4c5.jpg" width="540"/>
                         </figure>
                         <p>
                          <i>
                           <b>
                            Inks of certain colors are the most problematic.
                           </b>
                           (Photo: Getty Images)
                          </i>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          Whether its a skull, cross, rose, or moms name in a thorn bush wrapped around the bicep,
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://fivethirtyeight.com/datalab/how-many-people-regret-their-tattoos/" target="_blank">
                           about one quarter
                          </a>
                          of American adults have a tattoo. But a new study says about one out of every 10 inkers does not anticipate something that can come along with them: a severe, ongoing skin reaction.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          In the just-published
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.nyulangone.org" target="_blank">
                           NYU Langone Medical Center
                          </a>
                          research that studied tattoo-clad New Yorkers, those who reacted to tattoos experienced a rash, itching or swelling that lasted anywhere from four months to several years, with the longest-lasting complications stemming from ink shades of red and black.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          While we know infections are a risk of tattoos and can be dependent on tattoo parlor practices, a lot of the complications in our study  and that I have seen in my patients  do not have to do with the tattoo artist or parlor practices, but rather the qualities of ink and how the bodys immune system responds to it,
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://nyulangone.org/doctors/1144483843/marie-c-leger" target="_blank">
                           Marie C. Leger
                          </a>
                          , MD, PhD, study lead and Assistant Professor at the
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.med.nyu.edu/dermatology/ronald-o-perelman-department-dermatology" target="_blank">
                           Ronald O. Perelman Department of Dermatology
                          </a>
                          , tells Yahoo Health.
                         </p>
                         <figure>
                          <img data-src="https://s.yimg.com/cd/resizer/2.0/FIT_TO_WIDTH-w540/050d3beac46e3d619ec2dcc69a38785eb2473300.jpg" height="360" src="https://s.yimg.com/g/images/spaceball.gif" width="540"/>
                         </figure>
                         <p>
                          <i>
                           (Photo: Getty Images)
                          </i>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          The 300 respondents ranged in age from 18 to 69, with most having no more than five tattoos  and 67 percent of studied tattoos were on the arms. We were rather alarmed at the high rate of reported chronic complications tied to getting a tattoo. Given the growing popularity of tattoos, physicians, public health officials and consumers need to be aware of the risks involved, added Leger.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <b>
                           Related:
                           <i>
                            <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="https://www.yahoo.com/health/the-psychology-of-tattoos-demonstrated-by-rex-110094437652.html" target="_blank">
                             The Psychology of Tattoos, as Demonstrated by Rex Ryan
                            </a>
                           </i>
                          </b>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          While less than a third of the affected study participants saw a doctor for the reaction, the majority returned to the tattoo parlor to complain or ask for guidance. Tattoo artists are first responders when people have problems, says Leger, who adds that a planned follow-up study will examine what kind of reactions artists see most frequently, and how clients are directed, in an effort to get people to the right place for help.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          Experts are not surprised by the news. While tattoos are popular among Americans, there is still little to no regulation of what exactly is being injected into the skin, says
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.laserskinsurgery.com/Dermatologists/Jeremy-A-Brauer-MD" target="_blank">
                           Jeremy A. Brauer
                          </a>
                          , MD, dermatologist and Director of Clinical Research at Laser &amp; Skin Surgery Center of New York, where he has seen patients with tattoo reactions  most often to red ink. The majority of patients have allergic contact dermatitis, which is marked by redness, swelling and itching in the area of the tattoo.
                         </p>
                        </div>
                        <div class="post-cut W-100" id="post-cut" style="transition-property:none;-webkit-transition-property:none;transition-duration:10s;-webkit-transition-duration:10s;transition-timing-function:ease-in;-webkit-transition-timing-function:ease-in;overflow:hidden;max-height:0;max-width:500px;">
                         <p>
                          Treatment of tattoo reactions can be challenging says Brauer, who uses oral antihistamines combined with oral or injectable steroids to quell the inflammation. Blistering sometimes occurs and requires wound care and dressings, while evidence of infection is treated with antibiotics. More recently, there have been reports of successful laser treatment with both ablative and non-ablative fractional lasers, adds Bauer.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <b>
                           Related:
                           <i>
                            <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://news.yahoo.com/fda-warns-tattoo-inks-cause-infections-070516642--politics.html" target="_blank">
                             FDA Warns That Tattoos Can Cause Infection
                            </a>
                           </i>
                          </b>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          For some, the discomfort never completely goes away, and they are even driven to remove the tattoo altogether. While nanosecond (Q-switched) lasers have been the tattoo removal standard, the newer
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://picosure.com" target="_blank">
                           PicoSure
                          </a>
                          laser is now clearing tattoos in far fewer treatments than before, says Bauer. The laser also addresses and improves scarring that can be an accompanying issue with tattoos.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          And when it comes to tattoo removal, theres one design that stands out as the one most people want to get rid of: 52 percent of
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:3e62c43d-e078-3872-8eb5-b6cfc3df7f8b;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://trends.realself.com/2015/05/26/tattoo-removal-reviews-most-popular/" target="_blank">
                           RealSelf.com
                          </a>
                          doctors say tribal tattoos are the style they most frequently are asked to remove, for any number of reasons  from discomfort to regret.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          Looking ahead, Leger also has plans for a bigger survey to determine what tattoo dye components are most closely tied to adverse reactions. She hopes her investigation might also reveal other factors that put some people at higher risk of suffering chronic complications.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <b>
                           <i>
                            Lets keep in touch! Follow Yahoo Health on

                           </i>

                       </span>
                      </a>
                      <div class="item-text">
                       <div class="item-tag Tt-u Font-C-1-115 Fw-b" style="margin-left:175px;">
                        <a class="tag" data-action-outcome="navigate" data-ylk="g:6fb713f8-5a07-3f6a-a4f3-7a25fc1f2a88;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:also;ad:0;cpos:2;itc:0;t9:48;elm:itm;elmt:cat" href="/health/tagged/nutrition" style="color:#999;">

need more reasons to have sex, but how many guys actually stop to think about the fact that sex could play a key role in their overall health and well-being?\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Ci\u003E (Photo: Getty Images)\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERegular sexual activity is more than recreational; good sexual health is vital to a man&#x2019;s overall physical, psychological and emotional health. Sex can potentially benefit everything from a man&#x2019;s short-term mood, to longer-term issues like cardiovascular health and the reduced risk of prostate cancer.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn addition, sex is a cost-effective and enjoyable way to ensure your long-term health. It&#x2019;s cheaper than a gym membership and a lot better for your waistline than a nightly six pack. With the average man being more mindful than ever about both his budget and his health, sex might be just what the doctor ordered.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EHere are 5 reasons sex makes men healthier &#x2013; so get busy tonight.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ch2\u003E1. Calorie Burn\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E While sex alone may not be enough to create bulging&#xA0;biceps and washboard abs, intercourse definitely gets the blood pumping by raising the heart rate and burning calories. Estimates show that an average session between the sheets can burn 85 to upwards of 200 calories in 30 minutes. If you spent half an hour having sex daily, that could lead to a weight loss of 10 pounds or more over the course of a year. And it&#x2019;s hopefully more fun than a run on the treadmill. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated\u003Ci\u003E:&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.askmen.com\u002Ftop_10\u002Fdating\u002Fbest-sex-positions.html\"\u003E\u003Ci\u003ETop 10 Best Sex Positions\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003E2. Strength Training\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E In addition to burning calories, sex aids in testosterone production, which can strengthen bones and muscles. Testosterone is also responsible for maintaining healthy energy levels, mood, fertility, and sexual desire. So having sex today could help keep your sex drive up down the road. Decreasing testosterone levels, known as andropause or male menopause, can result in a number of side effects including decreased libido, fatigue and depression, among others.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated:\u003Ci\u003E \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.askmen.com\u002Fdating\u002Flove_tip\u002F59_love_tip.html\"\u003E7 Sexual Positions Women Enjoy \u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003E3. Cancer Prevention\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E A number of studies show that men who ejaculate on a regular basis are more than 30% less likely to develop&#xA0;prostate cancer. &#xA0;Regular ejaculation, whether from intercourse or masturbation, help flush out the prostate, which may help curb the development of prostate cancer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003E4. Improved Cardiovascular Health\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E Because sex is a good form of exercise, it makes sense that it would positively impact the health of your heart. A study from Queens University shows that men who have sex more than three times a week cut their risk of heart attack and stroke in half. Sex has also been shown to lower blood pressure, which further accounts for the overall positive impact on cardiovascular health. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated: \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.askmen.com\u002Ftop_10\u002Fdating\u002Fsex-basics.html\"\u003E\u003Ci\u003E10 Sex Basics To Up Your Bedroom Game\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003E5. Stress Relief\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E The decreased blood pressure that can result from sex could help lower your stress level. Various study participants have been shown to have a smaller rise in blood pressure during stressful situations after sex, as compared to other test subjects. Moreover, sex releases feel-good endorphins that can help lower anxiety. Bans in Soccer May Not be Enough to Stop Concussions","summary":"Outlawing heading the ball during soccer games may reduce the rate of concussions, but eliminating player-on-player contact would help more, suggests a new study.","body":"\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E\n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Fc7c7cfb8d4f97e864299648b66f12a9bc43b7b13.jpg\" alt=\"&#x2018;Heading&#x2019; Bans in Soccer May Not be Enough to Stop Concussions\" width=\"540\" height=\"810\"\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E\u003Ci\u003ELi Dongna of China and Alex Morgan of the United States go up for a header in the second half in the FIFA Women&#x2019;s World Cup 2015 Quarter Final match at Lansdowne Stadium on June 26, 2015 in Ottawa, Canada.\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Ci\u003E(Photo by Jana Chytilova\u002FFreestyle Photo\u002FGetty Images)\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOutlawing &#x201C;heading the ball&#x201D; during \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E games may reduce the rate of concussions, but eliminating player-on-player contact would help more, suggests a new study.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe findings challenge recent calls to ban &#x201C;heading,&#x201D; which is when players hit \u003Ca\u003Esoccer&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fa\u003Eballs with their heads, the study&#x2019;s lead author told Reuters Health.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nytimes.com\u002F2015\u002F07\u002F02\u002Fsports\u002Fsoccer\u002Fwomens-world-cup-semifinal-injuries-morgan-brian-alexandra-popp.html\"\u003Eheading debate\u003C\u002Fa\u003E sprang back into action after Morgan Brian of the U.S. and Alexandra Popp of Germany cracked heads while both attempting to head the ball in a Woman&#x2019;s World Cup semifinal game. Both were visibly injured, but deemed well enough to quickly return to playing in the game by their team doctors &#x2014; and \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nytimes.com\u002F2015\u002F07\u002F02\u002Fsports\u002Fsoccer\u002Fwomens-world-cup-semifinal-injuries-morgan-brian-alexandra-popp.html\"\u003Ereignited conversation\u003C\u002Fa\u003E that the&#xA0;F&#xE9;d&#xE9;ration Internationale de Football Association (FIFA) is not doing enough to protect its players.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;Intuitively it sounds great,&#x201D; said \u003Ca\u003EDawn Comstock\u003C\u002Fa\u003E of \u003Ca\u003EColorado School of Public Health\u003C\u002Fa\u003E at the \u003Ca\u003EUniversity of Colorado\u003C\u002Fa\u003E Anschutz Medical Campus in Aurora. &#x201C;It even has the word &#x2018;head&#x2019; in it.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EComstock and her colleagues report in JAMA Pediatrics that players are more likely to get concussions during heading than other \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E plays, but it&#x2019;s not the cause of the\u003Ca\u003Einjuries\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;When you look more deeply in the data, it&#x2019;s not the act of athletes hitting the balls with their heads at all,&#x201D; Comstock said. &#x201C;Rather, it&#x2019;s athlete-to-athlete contact that occurs during heading.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe Safer \u003Ca\u003ESoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E campaign, promoted by the nonprofit \u003Ca\u003EBoston\u003C\u002Fa\u003E-based \u003Ca\u003ESports Legacy Institute\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, urges that heading be delayed until high school or age 14. The campaign is backed by professional \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E players.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETo see if the data support a ban on heading, the researchers studied a representative sample of U.S. high school \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E players. Overall, between 2005 and 2014, 627 concussions occurred during 1,393,753 \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E practices or competitions.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EPlayer-on-player contact was the most common cause of concussions overall, they found.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHeading was the most common \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E-related play associated with concussions, compared to other plays like passing or defending the ball. Heading accounted for about 31 percent of concussions among boys and about a quarter of concussions among girls.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBut about 78 percent and about 62 percent of heading-relate concussions among boys and girls, respectively, were due to contact with another player.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile some young players do get concussions when they head the ball, that number is very small compared to those who are injured by another player while heading the ball, Comstock said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe current rules of \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E in many leagues reduce player-to-player contact, she said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;If we just enforce those kind of rules to eliminate or at least reduce athlete-to-athlete impacts, that would do more to reduce concussions than just banning heading alone,&#x201D; Comstock said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EDr. \u003Ca\u003ERobert Cantu\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, co-founder and medical director of the \u003Ca\u003ESports Legacy Institute\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, told Reuters Health that the new study confirms previous research on the rate of concussion during heading.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;What (Comstock) does point out - and she&#x2019;s looking at the high school level not the youth level - is that rough play also needs to be addressed,&#x201D; said Cantu, who was not involved with the new study. His organization supports that, he said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fhealth\u002Fhow-to-overcome-depression-after-a-sports-injury-92559471297.html\"\u003E\u003Ci\u003EHow to Overcome Depression After a Sports Injury\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHeading is still the activity with the highest incidence, he pointed out. Also, under age 14 is when the maximum development of the brain occurs.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;By banning headers before (age) 14 we can take 30 percent of concussions down to zero overnight,&#x201D; said Christopher Nowinski, the \u003Ca\u003ESports Legacy Institute\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#x2019;s co-founder and executive director.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EDr. \u003Ca\u003EJeffrey Bazarian\u003C\u002Fa\u003E of the \u003Ca\u003EUniversity of Rochester\u003C\u002Fa\u003E Medical Center in New York also cautioned that it would be difficult to know how many concussions could be prevented by enforcing current rules.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;When two players go up for a head ball, they&#x2019;re both following the rules,&#x201D; said Bazarian, who wasn&#x2019;t involved with the new research.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHe stressed that concussions are treatable, and the real question is how repeated hits to players&#x2019; heads impact them later on.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe authors also note out that most \u003Ca\u003Einjuries\u003C\u002Fa\u003E reported in \u003Ca\u003Esoccer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E are to lower parts of the body, not the head.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERead This Next:&#xA0;\u003Ci\u003E\u003Ca {"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F711e45012eb3aba60d4f9bbc1c1665fbdbfcbaca.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":500,"height":332,"size_type":"500","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002F711e45012eb3aba60d4f9bbc1c1665fbdbfcbaca.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":540,"height":359,"size_type":"original","formats":{}}],"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002Foriginal\u002FZpoxXPqvpCwft3Q2YWzcbfaBjSo","source_url":"http:\u002F\u002F36.media.tumblr.com\u002Ff96f21dfbb9cbd5434b5f4065a9dc1dd\u002Ftumblr_inline_nr8cvaG66X1t58yg4_540.jpg"}],"highlight_color":"#000000","data_version":"1","title":"One Womans Story Highlights theHidden Skin Cancer Risks for People of Color","summary":"When Jacqueline Smith discovered an almond-sized lump on her bikini line, the concerned college senior went to her campus health center to find out what it could be. She was told it was an inflamed hair follicle, but that was wrong.","body":"\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E\n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Fb4f536060e46b7a8df3ba09dab1e2d070fb8e3be.jpg\" alt=\"One Woman&#x2019;s Story Highlights the&#xA0;Hidden Skin Cancer Risks for People of Color\" width=\"540\" height=\"394\"\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003E\u003Cb\u003EWhile Caucasians are more likely to be diagnosed with skin cancer, only 16 percent of cases involve advanced-stage cancer. In comparison, 52 percent of African-Americans and 26 percent of Hispanic patients are diagnosed with late-stage cases.\u003C\u002Fb\u003E (Photo: Getty Images\u002FBrand X Pictures)\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhen Jacqueline Smith discovered an almond-sized lump on her bikini line, the concerned college senior went to her campus health center to find out what it could be. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere, she was told not to worry &#x2014; it was simply an inflamed hair follicle. But Jacqueline couldn&#x2019;t shake the feeling that the firm, unmoving lump was something more serious. After graduation, she headed back home to Hackensack, N.J., and saw her family&#x2019;s primary care physician for a second opinion. He referred her to a surgical oncologist, who biopsied the site and had dire news: Jacqueline had skin cancer, a stage IIIC melanoma that had metastasized and was just a whisper shy of slipping into stage IV cancer. &#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EA shocking diagnosis for anyone to handle, Jacqueline was especially incredulous: She was a young African-American woman with no family history of skin cancer, and she had never engaged in behaviors like tanning that studies show correlate with a higher risk.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;I was totally shocked by the diagnosis &#x2014; my first thought was, &#x2018;Melanoma is the \u003Ci\u003Edeadly\u003C\u002Fi\u003E skin cancer&#x2019; &#x2014; I never had a reason for it to be on my radar, and never thought I had to prevent it,&#x201D; Jacqueline tells Yahoo Health. &#x201C;I had always heard melanoma was a risk for fair-skinned, middle-aged Caucasian women.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E\n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" alt=\"image\" width=\"540\" height=\"359\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002F711e45012eb3aba60d4f9bbc1c1665fbdbfcbaca.jpg\"\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJacqueline Smith has survived two melanoma diagnoses. \u003C\u002Fb\u003E(Photo: Courtesy of Jacqueline Smith)\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EJacqueline, who&#x2019;s now working as the regional assistant for New York Sen. Kirsten Gillibrand, had the cancerous lymph nodes removed when she was just 21 years old. Young age and otherwise good health appeared to be on her side when subsequent scans showed no evidence of cancer, and doctors declared her clear to go on with life. Jacqueline excitedly began her first semester of a PhD program in sociology.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThen it happened again six years later, when she was 27 years old: Another lump emerged in the same area of her bikini line. Jacqueline visited the gynecologist at her university&#x2019;s health center, who said it was just an inflamed lymph node. &#x201C;If it doesn&#x2019;t bother you, don&#x2019;t bother it,&#x201D; advised the physician. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis time, Jacqueline wasted no time in seeking a second opinion. A needle biopsy confirmed the dreaded: Stage III melanoma had returned. &#x201C;Even though it was my second diagnosis, I was shocked all over again. My doctor told me, &#x2018;It will be a miracle if you survive another five years,&#x2019;&#x201D; shares Jacqueline.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EThe hidden skin cancer risks for people of color&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EPerhaps even more surprising is that Jacqueline&#x2019;s case isn&#x2019;t an anomaly. While Caucasians are more likely to be diagnosed with skin cancer, only 16 percent of cases involve advanced-stage cancer. In comparison, 52 percent of African-Americans and 26 percent of Hispanic patients are diagnosed with late-stage cases, according to the \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.skincancer.org\u002Fprevention\u002Fskin-cancer-and-skin-of-color\"\u003ESkin Cancer Foundation\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe initial site of Jacqueline&#x2019;s melanoma has never been determined. People of color are more likely to be diagnosed with melanoma in unusual areas like between the toes, under the nails, behind the ears, on the palms of hands and soles of feet, inside the lips, and between the buttock cheeks. Melanoma can even develop in the eyes, the lungs, the gastrointestinal tract, and the membrane known as the meninges that covers the brain and spinal cord.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E\u003Ci\u003ERelated: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fhealth\u002F3-tips-to-apply-sunscreen-the-right-way-you-know-119381099947.html\"\u003E3 Tips to Apply Sunscreen the Right Way\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;While we know UV light plays a role in melanomas found on Caucasian skin, the primary risk factors for melanoma in people with skin of color has yet to be determined,&#x201D; New York dermatologist \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.drwhitneybowe.com\"\u003EWhitney Bowe, MD\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, tells Yahoo Health. &#x201C;That&#x2019;s why patients with darker skin may get melanomas in areas that we don&#x2019;t commonly associate with sun exposure.&#x201D; \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis time around for Jacqueline, driving the cancer back into remission was grueling. She had all of her pelvic lymph nodes removed and underwent Interferon drug treatment and radiation over the course of a year that had her coping with painful open wound burns. While she finally did achieve remission, Jacqueline was left with lymphedema in one leg, a permanent severe swelling that is caused by lymphatic system blockage.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;I keep the lymphedema in perspective &#x2014; I have two legs that still work and I have my health, yet it&#x2019;s difficult being young and having so many restrictions. Once upon a time, just being able to choose my shoes was something I took for granted &#x2014; now, I can&#x2019;t fit into most regular shoes,&#x201D; she explains.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ELymphedema, which Jacqueline cites as her greatest survivor challenge, is a risk associated with lymph node removal after melanoma and breast cancer surgeries. &#x201C;The risk of developing lymphedema is dependent upon the number of lymph nodes removed &#x2014; so if melanoma is diagnosed early, there may be no lymph node dissection at all, and therefore no risk of lymphedema,&#x201D; says Deborah Lindquist, MD, an oncologist with Arizona Oncology, a practice in \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.usoncology.com\"\u003Ethe US Oncology Network\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Lindquist adds that a drug to treat the chronic condition is currently in clinical trials and may be available to the public in the near future.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAccording to the Skin Cancer Foundation, Hispanics account for the highest amount of new skin cancer cases among people of color. &#x201C;Melanoma incidence is increasing rapidly in Latinos, who are outpacing African-Americans likely due to more tanning bed usage,&#x201D; explains New Jersey dermatologist \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fimagedermatology.com\"\u003EJeanine B. Downie\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, MD. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EExperts recommend wearing sunscreen with an SPF of 30 or higher as the best way to prevent skin cancer and melanoma in people of any color. Jacqueline now follows the advice religiously; she always has sunscreen in her bag (favorites include sprays by \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.neutrogena.com\u002Fcategory\u002Fsun\u002Fsunscreen+spray.do\"\u003ENeutrogena\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;such as CoolDry Sport Sunscreen Spray and Ultra Sheer Body Mist Sunscreen), as she finds the spray formulas absorb without the chalkiness of other more traditional formulas. &#x201C;Please don&#x2019;t think you don&#x2019;t need sunscreen because your skin color is natural protectant from the sun &#x2014; anyone with skin is at risk for developing melanoma,&#x201D; she warns. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E\u003Ci\u003ERelated: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fhealth\u002Fthe-scary-causes-behind-the-rise-in-skin-cancer-118288125190.html\"\u003EThe Scary Causes Behind the Rise in Skin Cancer Rates\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMost importantly, experts say to get familiar with your skin, so that you notice any changes to new or existing spots and moles. Melanoma is virtually always curable if detected early. &#x201C;If the mole is multicolored, very dark, growing rapidly in size, painful, or stands out as totally different from your other moles, have a dermatologist check it out,&#x201D; Downie says. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHave an annual skin checkup with your dermatologist to assess new and old spots, and if you&#x2019;ve previously had precancerous or cancerous lesions, you should go even more regularly &#x2014; every six months. When visiting other doctors like your ophthalmologist and gynecologist, have them check for any unusual signs that you can&#x2019;t see, and ask the same of your hair stylist, who experts say is often your first line of defense against skin cancer on the scalp. &#xA0; &#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn fact, New York hair stylist Roman Kusayev of the \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fromanksalon.com\"\u003ERoman K Salon\u003C\u002Fa\u003E recently detected a melanoma on a regular Latina client&#x2019;s scalp while applying hair color. &#x201C;I noticed a suspicious slightly red spot on her scalp that clearly wasn&#x2019;t a wound or scab. I didn&#x2019;t want to scare her, but I knew I had the responsibility to bring it to her attention,&#x201D; Kusayev tells Yahoo Health. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe client soon made an appointment with her dermatologist, who diagnosed the lesion as skin cancer. Fortunately, the early detection made for routine removal. Hairstyles like tightly parted ponytails and tight braids can leave the scalp vulnerable to the sun&#x2019;s UV rays. Experts recommend wearing a hat or brushing hair back when out in the sun so as not to harshly part and expose swaths of scalp to UV rays. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIf you do see something pop up that gives you a funny feeling, Jacqueline says to always trust your instinct. &#x201C;It&#x2019;s important to be your own health advocate. I didn&#x2019;t stop harassing doctors until I definitively found out what was wrong, and that is what saved my life.&#x201D; \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E\u003Ci\u003ERead This Next: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fhealth\u002Fthe-part-of-your-face-you-absolutely-need-to-122973275813.html\"\u003EThe Part of Your Face You Absolutely Need to Protect From the Sun \u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003ELet&#x2019;s keep in touch! \u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F050d3beac46e3d619ec2dcc69a38785eb2473300.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":500,"height":333,"size_type":"500","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002F050d3beac46e3d619ec2dcc69a38785eb2473300.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":540,"height":360,"size_type":"original","formats":{}}],"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002Foriginal\u002FLpygwRqB6Ou1VHLABiTAhWtwmDs","source_url":"http:\u002F\u002Fmagazines.zenfs.com\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002Foriginal\u002FLpygwRqB6Ou1VHLABiTAhWtwmDs"}],"highlight_color":"#ffffff","data_version":"1","title":"Study: Tattoos Can Cause Serious, Long-Term, Adverse Reactions","summary":"Tattoo inks of certain colors are more problematic than others, according to a new study from NYU.","body":"\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E \n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002F13d7c4eccc3abebf6f93904fa38138c7711ce4c5.jpg\" width=\"540\" height=\"540\" alt=\"Study: Tattoos Can Cause Serious, Long-Term, Adverse Reactions\"\u003E \n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003E\u003Cb\u003EInks of certain colors are the most problematic.\u003C\u002Fb\u003E (Photo: Getty Images)\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhether it&#x2019;s a skull, cross, rose, or mom&#x2019;s name in a thorn bush wrapped around the bicep, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ffivethirtyeight.com\u002Fdatalab\u002Fhow-many-people-regret-their-tattoos\u002F\"\u003Eabout one quarter\u003C\u002Fa\u003E of American adults have a tattoo. But a new study says about one out of every 10 inkers does not anticipate something that can come along with them: a severe, ongoing skin reaction.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EIn the just-published \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nyulangone.org\"\u003ENYU Langone Medical Center\u003C\u002Fa\u003E research that studied tattoo-clad New Yorkers, those who reacted to tattoos experienced a rash, itching or swelling that lasted anywhere from four months to several years, with the longest-lasting complications stemming from ink shades of red and black.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;While we know infections are a risk of tattoos and can be dependent on tattoo parlor practices, a lot of the complications in our study &#x2014; and that I have seen in my patients &#x2014; do not have to do with the tattoo artist or parlor practices, but rather the qualities of ink and how the body&#x2019;s immune system responds to it,&#x201D; \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fnyulangone.org\u002Fdoctors\u002F1144483843\u002Fmarie-c-leger\"\u003EMarie C. Leger\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, MD, PhD, study lead and Assistant Professor at the \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.med.nyu.edu\u002Fdermatology\u002Fronald-o-perelman-department-dermatology\"\u003ERonald O. Perelman Department of Dermatology\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, tells Yahoo Health.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E \n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" width=\"540\" height=\"360\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002F050d3beac46e3d619ec2dcc69a38785eb2473300.jpg\"\u003E \n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003E(Photo: Getty Images)\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe 300 respondents ranged in age from 18 to 69, with most having no more than five tattoos &#x2014; and 67 percent of studied tattoos were on the arms. &#x201C;We were rather alarmed at the high rate of reported chronic complications tied to getting a tattoo. Given the growing popularity of tattoos, physicians, public health officials and consumers need to be aware of the risks involved,&#x201D; added Leger.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated: \u003Ci\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fhealth\u002Fthe-psychology-of-tattoos-demonstrated-by-rex-110094437652.html\"\u003EThe Psychology of Tattoos, as Demonstrated by Rex Ryan\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhile less than a third of the affected study participants saw a doctor for the reaction, the majority returned to the tattoo parlor to complain or ask for guidance. &#x201C;Tattoo artists are &#x2018;first responders&#x2019; when people have problems,&#x201D; says Leger, who adds that a planned follow-up study will examine what kind of reactions artists see most frequently, and how clients are directed, in an effort to get people to the right place for help.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EExperts are not surprised by the news. &#x201C;While tattoos are popular among Americans, there is still little to no regulation of what exactly is being injected into the skin,&#x201D; says \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.laserskinsurgery.com\u002FDermatologists\u002FJeremy-A-Brauer-MD\"\u003EJeremy A. Brauer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, MD, dermatologist and Director of Clinical Research at Laser &amp; Skin Surgery Center of New York, where he has seen patients with tattoo reactions &#x2014; most often to red ink. The majority of patients have allergic contact dermatitis, which is marked by redness, swelling and itching in the area of the tattoo.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ETreatment of tattoo reactions &#x201C;can be challenging&#x201D; says Brauer, who uses oral antihistamines combined with oral or injectable steroids to quell the inflammation. Blistering sometimes occurs and requires wound care and dressings, while evidence of infection is treated with antibiotics. More recently, there have been reports of successful laser treatment with both ablative and non-ablative fractional lasers, adds Bauer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated: \u003Ci\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fnews.yahoo.com\u002Ffda-warns-tattoo-inks-cause-infections-070516642--politics.html\"\u003EFDA Warns That Tattoos Can Cause Infection&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EFor some, the discomfort never completely goes away, and they are even driven to remove the tattoo altogether. While nanosecond (&#x201C;Q-switched&#x201D;) lasers have been the tattoo removal standard, the newer \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fpicosure.com\"\u003EPicoSure\u003C\u002Fa\u003E laser is now clearing tattoos in &#x201C;far fewer treatments than before,&#x201D; says Bauer. The laser also addresses and improves scarring that can be an accompanying issue with tattoos.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAnd when it comes to tattoo removal, there&#x2019;s one design that stands out as the one most people want to get rid of: 52 percent of \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftrends.realself.com\u002F2015\u002F05\u002F26\u002Ftattoo-removal-reviews-most-popular\u002F\"\u003ERealSelf.com\u003C\u002Fa\u003E doctors say tribal tattoos are the style they most frequently are asked to remove, for any number of reasons &#x2013; from discomfort to regret.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ELooking ahead, Leger also has plans for a bigger survey to determine what tattoo dye components are most closely tied to adverse reactions.
